<span id="freeTextContainer17342308530998257881">Illywhacker by Peter Carey<br/>Eight out of 10<br/><br/><br/>Although acclaimed and successful – the book was shortlisted for the Booker Prize and is found on The Guardian 1,000 Novels Everyone Must Read <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.theguardian.com/books/2009/jan/23/bestbooks-fiction'>https://www.theguardian.com/books/200...</a> - the undersigned had to stop at page 400 – out of 1101 on an ereader format.<br/><br/>The novel has merits, clearly, but on a subjective note the fact that it is about 600 pages long in hardcover is not one of them, since at a certain point the adventures of the protagonist seem to l</span>
  <span id="freeText17342308530998257881" style="display:none">Illywhacker by Peter Carey<br />Eight out of 10<br /><br /><br />Although acclaimed and successful – the book was shortlisted for the Booker Prize and is found on The Guardian 1,000 Novels Everyone Must Read <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.theguardian.com/books/2009/jan/23/bestbooks-fiction">https://www.theguardian.com/books/200...</a> - the undersigned had to stop at page 400 – out of 1101 on an ereader format.<br /><br />The novel has merits, clearly, but on a subjective note the fact that it is about 600 pages long in hardcover is not one of them, since at a certain point the adventures of the protagonist seem to lack the advertised humor – well, Illywhacker stops being so hilarious, if it ever was.<br />The hero is Herbert Badgery and we know right from the start that he is a liar; he has lived for more than one hundred and thirty years – if this is not the wrong data brought out from the memory – and therefore his account would be absurd.<br /><br />Hence, if you have an allergy – as is the case of yours truly for The Rhinoceros and the respective animals roaming through the streets – for what has to do with the absurd, then you may want to avoid this tome.<br />In this land, we are surrounded by absurdity, surreal decisions and troglodytes taking outlandish decisions, which is actually the situation in America now, where they have an orange moron with the “understanding of a fifth grade boy” taking decisions that affect the whole world.<br /><br />When dealing with that on a daily basis, one would like a change in reading material.<br />I do.<br /><br />Herbert aka Illywhacker flies an airplane in the first chapter, in 1919, when he meets Phoebe, daughter of Jack and Molly McGrath, a young woman who had had a lesbian relationship with Annette Davidson.<br />Phoebe is attracted by the hero, learns how to fly a plane and begins an intimate affair with the protagonist, who would like to ask Jack for her hand; only the latter is opposed to a relationship between people of very different ages, which is not exactly the case here.<br />There are some horny, hot scenes between Herbert and Phoebe, who have sex on the roof, using their imagination and giving in to their desires, in scenes that can be a pleasure to read.<br /><br />Illywhacker is thinking of building airplanes with his would be father-in-law, but the discussions with potential partners and investors do not go anywhere and Herbert has a clash with those who think about importing already made airplanes.<br />In fact, the hero has been showing this patriotism or nationalism when selling the ford model T, one of his clients, a farmer, was interested in buying the ford car, when the sales agent stated that this is not the car for him, he should better buy and Australian made model, even if not that good, it was domestically made.<br /><br />Jack commits suicide, Phoebe is pregnant but seems to fall for a rather outré poet, who works as a travelling salesman of sorts for a pharmaceutical enterprise, provides the woman with a potion that is supposed to provoke an abortion, which she wants, but I n results in convulsions and the mother nearing the brink of death.<br />The exasperated Illywhacker learns that she is poisoned, sends the enamored poet to fetch a doctor, and uses all his mental powers to make the physician arrive soon at the beck of his brain’s call.<br /><br />The poet arrives at the doctor’s home, but he is initially rejected for he looks like a homeless beggar and he has to force his way in, pushing the foot in the door, which is repeatedly kicked by the physician, up to the point where the frightened lover threatens that he would sue the doctor for every penny and have him disbarred by the Medical Association unless he comes right away.<br />It appears miraculous, but in spite of the terrible danger, the victim survives and she would give birth to a boy, and then insist on having a contract signed with her husband, thereby limiting their sexual intercourse to periods wherein the fertility was at its lowest, with the promise that he would take every precaution to avoid any further impregnation.<br /><br />Phoebe wants to fly the airplane and wants no more children, but she becomes pregnant again.<br />Critics have found this novel exhilarating, or to use a leitmotif from the phenomenal History of the Kelly Gang by the same author – adjectival.<br /><br />The undersigned has loved The True History of the Kelly Gang and Oscar and Lucinda, both remarkable works, winners of the Booker Prize and reviewed here:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://realini.blogspot.com/2015/11/true-history-of-kelly-gang-by-peter.html">http://realini.blogspot.com/2015/11/t...</a><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://realini.blogspot.com/2018/03/oscar-and-lucinda-by-peter-carey.html">http://realini.blogspot.com/2018/03/o...</a><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17342308530998257881" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2966639023" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2966639023" />
    <a title="Colin Davison" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11669483-colin-davison"><img alt="Colin Davison" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530782585p2/11669483.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2966639023?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Colin Davison" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Colin Davison" href="/user/show/11669483-colin-davison">Colin Davison</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571132072" href="/book/show/2449544.Illywhacker">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2966639023" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16016256247201073794">Amid dozens of fine reviews of Illywhacker and many interviews with its author, it’s remarkable that none have mentioned that the character of Illywhacker is based on the life of Peter Carey’s grandfather, Robert Graham Carey.<br/>Described at the time as a “hard-bitten pioneer,” the aviator flew his Morris Farman aircraft, decorated with the slogan “Who’s chicken, are you?”, on charter jobs and “barnstorming” joyrides, often marked by precarious take-offs and crash landings .<br/>It was Robert who bought</span>
  <span id="freeText16016256247201073794" style="display:none">Amid dozens of fine reviews of Illywhacker and many interviews with its author, it’s remarkable that none have mentioned that the character of Illywhacker is based on the life of Peter Carey’s grandfather, Robert Graham Carey.<br />Described at the time as a “hard-bitten pioneer,” the aviator flew his Morris Farman aircraft, decorated with the slogan “Who’s chicken, are you?”, on charter jobs and “barnstorming” joyrides, often marked by precarious take-offs and crash landings .<br />It was Robert who bought (not stole) and transported a church hall from Brighton as his home. The correspondences don’t end there. Robert was also involved with motoring – whether or not he sold model T Fords like Illywhacker I don’t know, but his son, Peter’s father, was a motor dealer.<br />And as for Illywhacker’s name, Herbert Badgery, he is surely named after Andrew Delfosse Badgery, reportedly the first man to fly an Australian-made plane, and who after World War 1 trained many other pilots.<br />Does any of this matter in assessing Carey’s remarkable novel? Perhaps not, but it made me wonder at least about Herbert Badgery assertion at the outset that he is “a terrible liar and I have always been a liar.”<br />His age, 139, is he says “the one thing that you can rely on.” And yet, paradoxically it is the one thing you realise is definitely untrue, as he reveals on the very next page. We have to wait until the penultimate of 600 tumultuous (hardback edition) pages to discover the basis of the lie.<br />Yes, Illywhacker lies from time to time. “I .. embellished (the truth) a little .. I danced around it like a bloody bower-bird putting on a display. I added silver to it. I put small blue stones around it,” he beautifully explains. But they are lies of a showman, lies to protect itinerant workers from police intimidation. <br />It is just that he is living in a country founded upon lies, a land stolen from its original owners, who were killed with a clear conscience, where convicts leave their thumbprints in its building bricks and in which its misappropriators “can only move around like tourists.”<br />The book is really three stories, linked as much by a political viewpoint as by the discontinuous presence of Illywhacker: the first the rumbustious tale of the unconventional anti-colonial narrator himself, vainly trying to establish Australian aircraft or motor manufacturing; he second of his socialist lover Leah; the third an allegory of a giant pet shop in which those exploiting captive species become captives themselves.<br />It’s about 150 pages too long, and Carey just cannot resist exploring every side-street of a backstory with the result that Leah’s history arrests the main narrative.<br />But with what amazing feats of imagination and language he dazzles and entertains. Carey writes as a cubist paints, with many-sided characters in scenes of intriguing, confusing complexity.<br />It is at times like the phoney alibis invented by Phoebe, another of Illwhacker’s lovers, “half-formed jelly-like things with no proper legs or faces.” Yet at the same time there is a real feeling of an Australia, at a time it is outgrowing one national consciousness and not yet finding its own. Ballarat, Bendigo, Geelong – these are places I know as a visitor today, but here they are, with post Gold-rush villages and diggers with cardboard for shoes.<br />Illywhacker does not seem to me to an unreliable narrator. He is too honest about his lies for that, and the detail too particular, such as the widow’s dress hastily dyed black for mourning – leaving black marks on her fingers.<br />He passes bad cheques, we learn from another, and he’s economical with the truth – about his first wife, for example. His accounts of disappearing tricks, making a dragon (a euphemism for telling lies, we learn) or the fabulous reincarnations of a severed finger in a bottle can only be tall stories told by an old man losing the desire to distinguish fantasy and reality.<br />Such splashes of magical realism are sometimes overdone and don’t add a lot to the thrust of the story, or as Howard Jacobson wrote: “There is an overfertility of metaphor, one dragon and one traveling Australian illusionist too many.”<br />Yet I love his verdict that reading 'Illywhacker “is not unlike spending a week in the company of the best kind of Australian. The stories keep coming, told with deceptive guilelessness and innocence.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="16016256247201073794" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jayant Maini" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15528840-jayant-maini"><img alt="Jayant Maini" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1355665400p2/15528840.jpg" /></a>

<span id="freeTextContainer9048543510080506989">This is my second book by Peter Carey (first one was Oscar and Lucinda) and i would say that i am deeply unsatisfied........even though his characters are great and have that magical quality....they seemed to be forced..........!! I always feel that there is something missing in his novels.......!!! I am ready to accept that i might lack the expertize to give any opinion on his work........but i would say that the two of his novels O and L and Illywhacker, that i read, left me discontented. </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Ritchie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Ritchie" href="/user/show/36583554-michael-ritchie">Michael Ritchie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16653298189914053023">First time in years I've stopped a book without finishing it. The writing is rather lovely, and there are some great turns of phrase, but the plot is so uninteresting to me that I can't be doing with nearly 600 pages of the stuff. I read about a third of it, but I've too much else to read right now. Maybe I'll return to it and finish it off sometime, but for now, no.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('pqzfTTsWvaZ18UDzByrqo2eQ9zHwhObsbgnuoosthjl3IyJrK1r00gd7MYIkNgz/x/uZHF6cGk2WFDWmbhkckQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2XeRM5fWjOZ1fBoWYCeDoImryqw2yUoG2C0g6hTSmCoI+GwVh5rFkgf2a2dDO2X8KcCkgZjRtqcgMPvu8eYCgg==')})
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_35306'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35306.Dirt_Music?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Dirt Music<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/19929.Tim_Winton?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Tim Winton<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.86 avg rating &mdash; 11,898 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2002\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer3668680523497959284\">Luther Fox, a loner, haunted by his past, makes his living as an illegal fisherman, a shamateur. Before everyone in his family was killed in a freak rollover, he grew melons and played guitar in the family band. Robbed of all that, he has turned his <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText3668680523497959284\" style=\"display:none\">Luther Fox, a loner, haunted by his past, makes his living as an illegal fisherman, a shamateur. Before everyone in his family was killed in a freak rollover, he grew melons and played guitar in the family band. Robbed of all that, he has turned his back on music. There\'s too much emotion in it, too much memory and pain. One morning Fox is observed poaching by Georgie Jutland. Chance, or a kind of willed recklessness, has brought Georgie into the life and home of Jim Buckridge, the most prosperous fisherman in the area and a man who loathes poachers, Fox above all. But she\'s never fully settled into Jim\'s grand house on the water or into the inbred community with its history of violent secrets. After Georgie encounters Fox, her tentative hold on conventional life is severed. Neither of them would call it love, but they can\'t stay away from each other no matter how dangerous it is, and out on White Point it is very dangerous.  Set in the dramatic landscape of Western Australia, Dirt Music is a love story about people stifled by grief and regret; a novel about the odds of breaking with the past and about the lure of music. Dirt music, Fox tells Georgie, is &quot;anything you can play on a verandah or porch, without electricity.&quot; Even in the wild, Luther cannot escape it. There is, he discovers, no silence in nature.  Ambitious, perfectly calibrated, Dirt Music resonates with suspense and supercharged emotion, and it confirms Tim Winton\'s status as the preeminent Australian novelist of his generation.<\/span>\n       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/22595.Peter_Carey">About Peter Carey</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/22595.Peter_Carey"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1334883386p3/22595.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/22595.Peter_Carey">Peter Carey
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
844
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9666085246494360147"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show_group/44217.Master_List_of_Author_Disambiguation_by_Spaces' rel='nofollow noopener'>See this thread for more information</a>.</i><br/><br/>Peter Carey was born in Australia in 1943. <br/><br/>He was educated at the local state school until the age of eleven and then became a boarder at Geelong Grammar School. He was a student there between 1954 and 1960 — after Rupert Murdoch had graduated and before Prince Charles arriv</span>
  <span id="freeText9666085246494360147" style="display:none"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show_group/44217.Master_List_of_Author_Disambiguation_by_Spaces" rel="nofollow noopener">See this thread for more information</a>.</i><br /><br />Peter Carey was born in Australia in 1943. <br /><br />He was educated at the local state school until the age of eleven and then became a boarder at Geelong Grammar School. He was a student there between 1954 and 1960 — after Rupert Murdoch had graduated and before Prince Charles arrived. <br /><br />In 1961 he studied science for a single unsuccessful year at Monash University. He was then employed by an advertising agency where he began to receive his literary education, meeting Faulkner, Joyce, Kerouac and other writers he had previously been unaware of. He was nineteen. <br /><br />For the next thirteen years he wrote fiction at night and weekends, working in many advertising agencies in Melbourne, London and Sydney. <br /><br />After four novels had been written and rejected The Fat Man in History — a short story collection — was published in 1974. This slim book made him an overnight success. <br /><br />From 1976 Carey worked one week a month for Grey Advertising, then, in 1981 he established a small business where his generous partner required him to work only two afternoons a week. Thus between 1976 and 1990, he was able to pursue literature obsessively. It was during this period that he wrote War Crimes, Bliss, Illywhacker, Oscar and Lucinda. Illywhacker was short listed for the Booker Prize. Oscar and Lucinda won it. Uncomfortable with this success he began work on The Tax Inspector. <br /><br />In 1990 he moved to New York where he completed The Tax Inspector. He taught at NYU one night a week. Later he would have similar jobs at Princeton, The New School and Barnard College. During these years he wrote The Unusual Life of Tristan Smith, Jack Maggs, and True History of the Kelly Gang for which he won his second Booker Prize. <br /><br />He collaborated on the screenplay of the film Until the End of the World with Wim Wenders. <br /><br />In 2003 he joined Hunter College as the Director of the MFA Program in Creative Writing. In the years since he has written My Life as a Fake, Theft, His Illegal Self and Parrot and Oliver in America (shortlisted for 2010 Man Booker Prize). <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9666085246494360147" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1461458-illywhacker">Quotes from Illywhacker</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Charles loved her voice. It was so soft and blurred, like pastels. It made his neck tingle just to listen to her. It gave him the same delicious feeling he had as he hovered on the brink of sleep and this feeling - until now - had been the single most pleasant feeling in his life. It was the voice that coloured everything he now thought about her. It was shy and tentative and musical. Sometimes he did not manage to hear the words she said, but he did not let on about his deafness.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/601976-charles-loved-her-voice-it-was-so-soft-and-blurred">5 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;My name is Herbert Badgery. I am a hundred and thirty-nine years old and something of a celebrity. They come and look at me and wonder how I do it. There are weeks when I wonder the same, whole stretches of terrible time. It is hard to believe you can feel so bad and still not die.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
